BATTLESHIPS BY WIRE
Yes, battleships by wire! Tanks, guns — all the implements of war come by
wire. Such things are shaped and forged and welded by that great, un¬
seen ally—electricity!
Through a wire it flows ... to make iron run like water, swing a hundred
ton beam, stamp out metal, speed up work of men by providing eye¬
saving light ... do a thousand tasks, serve mightily in a thousand ways.
We, of your Municipal Electric Plant, welcome our opportunity to provide
electricity for war and civilian uses throughout the area we serve. We
have what it takes to see that electricity does its full share in equipiping
our fighters adequately for VICTORY!
OUR NO. 1 JOB . . . HELP WIN THE WAR
BUY ALL THE WAR SAVINGS STAMPS AND BONDS
YOU POSSIBLY CAN!
CITY ELECTRIC DEPARTMENT
George A. Pierce, Commissioner of Utilities
Utility Building
Jacksonville, Florida

It is with a real feeling of gratitude and satisfaction that the Fortieth
Annual Year Book and Directory of the Jacksonville Jewish. Center
is published. This publication is not merely a Yearbook. It is a con¬
crete sample of the American way of life. It is a record of the growth
of a religious institution. Jews and non-Jews have contributed to the
success of this book and we wish to thank all of them, advertisers and
well wishers.
As long as it is possible for a Religious Institution in a typical Amer¬
ican community to gain the support and understanding of business
firms and individuals who belong to other faiths and who come from
different national backgrounds, so long will American democracy be
strong, and the American way of life shall emerge victorious from the
present struggle.
S. H. Rosenberg:
This book is divided into four main divisions. The first division describes the organization of the Center. The |
| second division describes the religious, educational and recreational activities of the institution. The third division ¡
¡ tells of the services the Center performs for the community at large. The fourth division is devoted to the Hebrew- 1
1 English calendar, greetings from friends, and the directory of advertisers. |
We know that this book will be a valuable guide. And we hope that just as this book is larger than the previous ¡
| one because of its increased information, advertisers and well wishers, so will the next year’s book show an even I
| greater increase in the functions of the Center to the Jewish community and the community at large.
At this time it is our pleasure to publicly thank the men and women who worked sincerely and long hours to pre- |
¡ pare this book for the printer. It is no exaggeration to say that without the unusual abilities of Mr. Dave Gershon, our |
¡ advertising manager, this publication would not be what it is. Three years ago Mr. Gershon came to the Center to |
I act as advertising manager. He brought with him many years of experience in the advertising field. During these three ¡
| years, the Yearbook has become a symbol of good will and an excellent medium for advertising. Thank you, Mr. Gershon, |
| for your excellent work. |
| The columns and pages of greetings found in this book are due to the fine work of Miss Ethel Joel. They are a |
| silent testimony to her very many friends. We are grateful to Miss Joel for her devoted work. |
Miss Miriam Spitz, the Center secretary, was ready at all times to give of her information, her ability and of her * ¡
| time to make this Yearbook complete and correct. Our heartfelt thanks to you, Miss Spitz, for your part in this work. |
The loyalty of Mr. Hyman Selber to the Center, and to this Yearbook as an expression of the Center, has been of \
| great value. His suggestions and criticisms have helped th e Editorial Board at every step. May you continue your |
| work, Mr. Selber, for many more years. g
Thus another Yearbook is presented to the Jacksonville community. Let us hope and pray that next year’s Year- ¡
j book will be devoted to the victorious and just peace which will follow the war. j

The Synagogue is one of the
finest examples of enduring and
progressive human institutions. It
has a history which reaches back
2500 years, it has been the pat¬
tern for the Christian Church and
the Mohammedan Mosque, and is
still today the most important in¬
stitution in Jewish life.
The Synagogue has endured,
and will continue to endure, be¬
cause it embodies the past and
present experiences and tradi¬
tions of the Jewish people, be¬
cause it is able to adjust itself
to the every-changing experiences
of new generations. The Syna¬
gogue was not imposed upon the
Jewish people, it was not the
‘‘brain child” of one or more
“planners” who were going to
give the people something good—
the Synagogue in its present form
is the result of 25 centuries of
growth, the Synagogue came from
the people, it is the creation of
the Jewish masses.
Historically it is difficult to
determine when the first Syna¬
gogue made its appearance. It
is known, however, that it had its
beginnings in Babylonia about
2500 years ago. The First Temple
was destroyed, the Jews were
exiled from their land, and their
traditions and their observances
were badly uprooted. The Baby¬
lonian Jews sought companion¬
ship, they wanted to study the
Holy Books, they longed for the
rituals and observances associated
with the Temple. In response to
these needs the Synagogue
emerged. Jews would gather in
the homes of their leaders to
worship together, to chant pray¬
ers and hymns, to listen to ^a
learned Jew reading a chapter or
two from the Bible and explain¬
ing it; they gathered to share
their joys, their sorrows, their
laughter and their tears.
And even during the Second
Temple this democratic institu¬
tion continued to grow and to
expand. True, worship through
sacrifices and offerings were con¬
centrated in the Temple proper.
The Synagogue, however, became
the Beth Ham-Midrash, the House
of Study.
During the days of the Second
Temple, Synagogues were flour¬
ishing everywhere, in Palestine

and outside of Palestine. With the
destruction of the Temple in the
year 70, the Synagogue took upon
itself another function—it now
also served as a Beth Ha-Tefillah,
the House of Prayer.
During the middle ages the
Synagogue once again demon¬
strated that it was the concrete
embodiment of the past and
present experiences and traditions
of the Jewish people, that it was
able to adjust its functions to the
new needs of new generations,
that it was a democratic institu¬
tion created by the people and for
the people. During this dreary
period of Jewish life, the Syna¬
gogue became the Beth Ha-
Keneseth, the House of As¬
sembly. Thus the Synagogue was
at this time not alone a place
for worship or study, it was the
meeting place for all Jews. Of
course these three functions of
the Synagogue did not develop as
rapidly or as simply as this brief
outline may indicate, it reached
these expressions only after cen¬
turies of continued existence.
Today the Synagogue is faced
with the necessity of making an¬
other adjustment. It can no
longer serve only religious needs,
it can no longer serve only as a
House of Study, nor can it serve
as a House of Assembly in the
narrow sense of the term. The
Synagogue today must become
the expression of the entire
Jewish community, it must pro¬
vide fellowship, religion and
study for all kinds of Jews and
for all age levels. Arid many
Synagogues have successfully
made this adjustment. The Syna¬
gogue Center provides educa¬
tional and1 recreational activities
for all of its meinbers and for the
community at large; community
Forums, athletic activities, plays,
concerts, art exhibits, men’s clubs,
women’s organizations, clubs, li¬
braries, social activities, etc. — all
these are the new services of the
Synagogue Center and only the
synagogues which will respond
and adjust to these needs will
flourish. And only by such ad¬
justments will Judaism continue
to be the most important element
in Jewish life.

===== PAGE 11 =====
Mr. Abe Newman
This Yearbrook is dedicated to Mr. Abe Newman, our esteemed
President, in grateful appreciation for his sincere devotion to and tireless
work for the Jacksonville Jewish Center and for the Jewish community
at large. His high ethical standards, his simplicity and good sense, his
Jewish zeal and his fine leadership have endeared him to all.
It is indeed a rare blessing for a community to have a leader of his fine
qualities. Mr. Newman’s personality kept in balance the various conflict¬
ing tendencies that are sure to develop in an institution that serves both
Conservative and Orthodox elements.
His personal integrity and honesty of purpose won for him the
respect and admiration of all Center members. He was, therefore, able
almost single handed to conduct a drive for the retirement of the large
Center mortgage which after months of hard and persistent effort was
crowned with complete success.
The heavy burden under which the institution had been laboring all
these years was suddenly lifted enabling the Center to scan new horizons
of achievements in serving the religious, educational and social needs of
the Jewish community.
With almost equal zeal Mr. Newman entered into the annual drive
of the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council shouldering with its presi¬
dent a great deal of the responsibility for the most successful campaign
in the history of Jacksonville Jewry.
Mr. Newman, therefore, well deserves the recognition given him when
he was overwhelmingly elected Jacksonville’s representative to the Ameri¬
can Jewish Conference.
This dedication is only a small token of the affection that fills the >
hearts of the members of the Center for Mr. Abe Newman. On the occasion
of the New Year we pray for his well-being and wish him and his dear
ones the blessing of good health, prosperity and happiness.

Mr. Simon Selber — Died Nisan 13, 5703
5
The membership of the Jacksonville Jewish Center announced the
loss of one of its devoted members and friends, Mr. Simon Selber.
Mr. Selber was a simple and God-fearing man and lived at peace
with his fellow human beings. He was a good father and a good husband.
He was a zealous worker for the Center and was vitally interested
in the welfare of his people, his religion and its spiritual heritage.
With loving care he supervised the Center building and painstak¬
ingly worked for its physical and aesthetic improvement. The Center will
be forever grateful for the years he has devoted to the improvement and
beautification of the Center Cemetery.
Mr. Selber’s devotion to the synagogue has inspired the loyalty of
his children who became indefatiguable workers for the Center and prom¬
inent leaders in the community.
His memory will live eternally in the hearts of all who knew him
and his spirit will never depart from the Center where he worked so hard
and faithfully.
We thank God for the life of Simon Selber. May his soul be bound
up in the bond of life eternal.
5

===== PAGE 14 =====
Message of the President
I am very happy to report that the Center has
*■
progressed along the lines of finance, education,
religion, recreation and community service. The pages
of this book will describe this growth. Permit me,
however, to emphasize this one thought - it is very
easy to become satisfied with the progress that has
been made, it is easier to slide backward than to move
forward. To hold the gains that have been made, will
require your support and cooperation as a worker and
as a contributor. It is not sufficient to provide
religious needs - you and your children must partici¬
pate in them. It is not sufficient to supply educa¬
tional facitilies - you and your children must use them.
Nor is it sufficient to arrange recreational activi¬
ties - you and your children must find a need for them.
Only in this way will the Jewish Center grow and expand
its functions, only with this cooperation will we be
successful.
Let us pray that the New Year will bring peace to all
the world, and that the United Nations will emerge the
victors, and then we will move forward towards even
greater accomplishments. May God grant you all a very
Happy New Year.
ABE NEWMAN.

The outstanding characteristic of
the High Holidays is that they con¬
cern themselves primarily with the
Jew as an individual. All other fes¬
tivals have either national or agri¬
cultural origins, and serve to
strengthen the bends between the
Jew, his people and its past. Rosh
Hashonah and Yom Kippur bring
home to the individual Jew the
meaning of his daily struggle for ex¬
istence, his relationship to his fel¬
low human beings and to God, the
Creator and Ruler of the Universe.
The great hold that these Days of
Awe (Yomim Noroim) have upon
us in this age of irreligión may be
partially explained by the fact that
the ideals and emotions stimulated
by them probe deeply into the pri¬
vate life of every Jew and lift him
to a high spiritual level.
If the ritual of introspection and
self-purification of these solemn
days were accepted by the world at
large, it would bring inestimable
good to humanity and help to dis¬
solve the world’s ills.
In a world corrupted by greed,
hatred and bloodshed, Rosh Hash¬
onah and Yom Kippur, with their
call to repentance, their prayers for
universal peace and the establish¬
ment of the Kingdom of God on
earth offer a valuable message to
humanity.
Rosh Hashonah
Rosh Hashonah aims to improve
human nature. The prayers, the rit¬
ual, the music, the sounding of the
Shofar are calculated to elicit from
the worshippers the finest and high¬
est expressions of his personality.
They impress upon him the vanity
and uselessness of envy, greed and
hate, and stimulate in him emotions
of tolerance, love and the proper
understanding of the real values of
life.
During these days, God, as it
were, sits in judgment over us, re¬
viewing our good and bad deeds.
This, more concretely, takes form of
every individual searching his inner
self and resolving to eliminate that
which is cruel and ugly in his life.
Tin New Year ritual prepares him

to meet the coming year cleansed
in spirit and determined to follow
the path of good life so that he may
find favor in the eves of God and
j
man.
Yom Kippur
One of the prime functions of
Yom Kippur is to break down the
natural resentment on the part of
human beings to admit that they
are wrong. The solemn Kol Nidre
service is opened with a petition to
God that he may permit us to pray
in the company of the transgres¬
sors, a term which may be applic¬
able to every member in the con¬
gregation. The public recitation of
the long catalogue of sins, among
which we are sure to find some that
apply to ourselves, the request for
forgiveness, which fill the prayer-
book, the custom of asking forgive¬
ness from those whom we know
that we have wronged, all of these
are calculated to bring about a
mood in which the worshipper ac¬
knowledges his errors and sins. This
is the first step necessary in making
any improvements, for without
acknowledging error there can be
no correction of error.
Yom Kippur also serves to dem¬
onstrate the supremacy of the spirit
over the flesh. Twenty four hours of
fasting is not only a denial of the
natural hungers of the body but
also a test of a man’s willpower to
withstand the temptations to which
he may be exposed in the year to
come.

M
MORRIS WOLFSON
We are happy to pay this tribute to Mr. Morris Wolfson. Both as
chairman of the Mortgage Clearance Drive and as a contributor, he was
greatly instrumental in the retirement of the Center Mortgage. His loyal
support of our president and his generosity gave the drive the impetus
it needed to assure its success.
p§
Due to the active interest of men like Mr. M. Wolfson, the Center
will ever grow and be a source of pride to the Jews and to the community
at large. May he continue his activities for the Center and Judaism. May
he and his beloved ones be granted many years of life, good health and
happiness.

Heirs of a Great Spiritual Heritage
By MORRIS D. MARGOLIS
Rabbi of Jacksonville Jewish Center
As the year 5703 is drawing to a close, the democratic
| world and the Jewish people in particular, have good rea-
3 son to look more hopefully into the future.
| During the past three years a monstrous and brutal
§ war machine has rolled across many countries destroying
| everything in its path. It registered victory after victory
| and it seemed that its power was inexhaustable. An era
| of darkness and slavery faced the world, which according
1 to one boast, was to last a thousand years.
| But as the might of the United Nations has made it-
| self felt with increasing strength, Hitler’s dream of world
| conquest now is crumbling in the dust.
In recent months the triumphs in North Africa and
§ Sicily, and the growing revolts in the occupied countries,
1 all point to the ultimate destruction of Nazism and
| Fascism, the twin evils of modern civilization.
With the day of liberation in sight the remaining Jews
i of Europe take courage in the hope that it may yet come
I in time to save them. The dark clouds are brightening.
s
At the same time the issue o£ the White Paper re-
| mains. The next few months will decide the course of
3 history, so far as the Zionist movement is concerned.
I Either we will be permitted by the Mandatory Power—
3 Great Britain—to continue rebuilding our ancient home-
I land or we will be cast off as an old shoe.
What has happened is familiar history to our people. 3
Over-confidence in its own strength, an insatiable desire |
for more and more conquests, unwillingness to tolerate the |
existence of any other power—these have brought ruin |
to military nations of the past. These blind passions are |
also sure to bring the downfall of the despotic conquerers 3
of our day. The road to disaster is clearly marked for If
those who would dominate the world by the power of the g
sword. g
The Jew, whose memory is long, and who has wit- g
nessed the rise and fall of many tyrants, cannot but have =
complete confidence in the defeat of the enemies of God =
and man. With the Psalmist, he reaffirms his faith in the §
supremacy of the spirit. “Some trust in war chariots, 1
some in horses but we shall put our trust in the name of i
the Lord.” The conviction is growing in the hearts of man 1
that righteousness and justice, kindnesss and decency shall 1
prevail; that evil shall be destroyed, that a just and peace- J
ful society shall ultimately be established, based on the 3
ethical and moral principles of the Law of God. =
On Rosh Hashonah, we Jews, heirs to a great spiritual |
heritage, gather in our synagogues to give expression |
through our prayers and songs to this hope which still 1
lives in the hearts of millions of men already over- |
whelmed by a brutal power. May our petitions be ac- 1
ceptable to God. May He send redemption to our people |
and to all mankind during the coming year. Amen.

MESSAGE OF DIRECTOR
S. H. ROSENBERG
As I leaf through the pages of this Yearbook for 1943-44 and as I rapidly review the accomplishments
and even the shortcomings of the past year, I feel that a tribute is due to the Center member and to the Center
Board member.
It is not my purpose to review here the accomplishments of the past year—you will find them briefly out¬
lined in the pages of this book. Nor is it my purpose to ennumerate the shortcomings of the past year—those
shortcomings and the recommendations for their cor lection will be found in the minutes of the annual meet¬
ing of the Congregation.
I do know, however, that in the final analysis it is the willingness of an institution to move forward that
makes for success, and if, as your agent, I have attained any measure of success it was due to the Center mem¬
ber. It was your cooperation which resulted in an increase of 100 new members; it was your generosity
which successfully cleared the Center of its mortgage indebtedness; it was your interest that made the daily
Nursery Kindergarten successful; it was your farsightedness that brought about budgeted expenditures and
income; and it was your sincere concern for Jewish education which enabled us to buy a new Center school
bus. I want to thank you for your devotion and your cooperation.
If you have not participated in these accomplishments, it has been your loss and the Center s loss. You
can make your Center an important community agency, you can make your Center dignified and progressive,
you can make your Center a potent influence in the education, recreation and religion of your community and
of the South. The Center has the potentialities of becoming a great and outstanding institution—your criti¬
cisms and your suggestions are always welcome.
The Daughters of Israel also deserve a special tribute. I have had many opportunities to meet with
them during the past year and I know of their wholehearted interest in the Center. Whenever they were
called upon to help the Center, whether financially or otherwise, they were ready and willing to respond.
They helped generously in the mortgage drive and were instrumental in the buying of the new school bus. I
know that they will continue their fine work, and I also know that they will take an even greater and more
direct interest in all matters pertaining to the Center.
The work of an Executive Director is largely dependent upon the cooperation of the people with whom
he is working. And I am happy to say that it has been a real privilege and pleasure to work with the Center
members, the Hebrew School teachers, the Sunday School staff, the Board members, and the office worker. I
thank them all for their whole-hearted support of all your undertakings.
Let us pray that the new year will bring a speedy and just peace to all the world and that Eretz Yisroel
will once again be the Land of Israel.

(From the Annual Report for the Year Ending May, 1943)
It is only through the untiring effort of our President, Mrs. Isadore
Gartner, and the sincere cooperation which she has received from the
officers, Board members, and the entire membership at large, that we,
the Daughters of Israel, can look back with pride upon our accomplishments
this past year.
Mrs. I. Gartner was assisted by Mrs. Louis Witten, Mrs. Perry Safer
and Mrs. Philip Selber, first, second and third vice-presidents, respectively.
Mrs. Haskell Zimmerman served as recording secretary. Mrs. Wm. Gordon,
corresponding secretary, performed her duties capably and Mrs. Morris
Hammerman was very efficient as financial secretary. Mrs. Florence Bono
was elected to serve as treasurer for the 12th consecutive year. However,
due to her untimely death, she was succeeded by her daughter, Mrs. Joe
Blattner, who, in a short time, has proven her capabilities.
Mrs. I. Gartner
The following standing committees worked diligently at their respective jobs:
The Religious Committee, composed of Mrs. J. Krestul, Mrs. M. D. Margolis and Mrs. O. Magezis, has kept us in¬
formed of the acivities of the Hebrew School and Sunday School. They saw to it that appropriate arrangements
were made for every holiday and that gifts were presente d to the outstanding students of the Sunday and Hebrew
schools. A word of thanks is due Mrs. Max Mirkis for the Children’s Seder, held on the Sunday before Passover.
Mrs. Louis Witten served as chairman of our Membership Committee. She and her committee were responsible
for the enlistment of 35 new members.
The cards and wires which our members and frien ds have received for the past few years on joyous occa¬
sions as well as on sad occasions, was the sole responsibility of Mrs. Sam Bryan, our Sunshine Chairman.
The flowers placed in the urn that have so beautifully decorated our synagogue on Friday nights and on vari¬
ous holidays were donated by our members to celebrate birthdays, anniversaries, Bar Mitzvahs and to commemorate
yarzheit. Mrs. P. Haimowitz and Mrs. Louis Goldberg, w ho were in charge of this activity, saw to it that after serv¬
ices these flowers were distributed to sick members or hospital wards.
Ü Mrs. Hyman Selber, in her usual capable manner, has been most successful in obtaining hostesses for the
1 social hour held after the late Friday night services.
| Mrs. Bennie Baker did a fine job in procuring hostesses for the social hour following the monthly meeting.
The Cultural chairman, Mrs. M. J. Reiser, has presented programs following our meetings which have proven
| to be both educational and enjoyable. Her untiring efforts with the dramatic group are highly appreciated.
= To Mrs. Charles Fink, chairman, and Mrs. Cele Schemer, co-chairman, are due a round of applause for
| the splendid piece of work they have accomplished this past year in sewing for the Red Cross, under the auspices
| of the Daughters of Israel. Mrs. Fink and her women also made a very beautiful altar cloth for the prayer room,
| and they hemmed 16 tablecloths for the use of the Center.
Mrs. Perry Safer, chairman, Mrs. A. E. Selber and Mrs. Ben Dwoskin, co-chairman, of the Telephone Com-
| mittee, responded graciously whenever called upon.
1 The publicity which appeared in both local papers was prepared by Mrs. Haskell Zimmerman.
We are indeed proud of our Girl Scout Troop and their dynamic Scout leader, Mrs. Louis Slott. It is through
| her leadership that the troop has grown and made progress.
When we at the Center think of preparing and serving food, our thoughts immediately go to Miss Ethel Joel,
our Catering Chairman. She is assisted by Mrs. Olga Moscovitz as co-chairman and a capable committee.
The Daughters of Israel, ever ready to assume new obligations, has not shirked its share in planning and enter¬
taining for our men in uniform. We have served as hostesses approximately once a month at a dance, at which
time nothing was spared to make the evening or afternoon most enjoyable for our boys. Upon the shoulders of
Mrs. Max Mirkis, Mrs. Haskell Zimmerman and Mrs. M. J. Silberstein, fell the greatest amount of the responsi-
1 bility. They are due a vote of thanks.
Last, but not least, a word of praise is due Mrs. A. Safer, who has been appointed to serve on the Tuberculosis
Association Board for the past few years. This honor w as bestowed upon her in recognition of the wonderful
work that she and her group of women from the Daughters of Israel have done in assisting in the sale of Tuber¬
culosis seals.
Had world conditions been normal, this year would h ave seen the Daughters of Israel celebrate their 20th an¬
nual cabaret with a gala affair.. However, we were forced to deviate from our traditional procedure and plan
accordingly. Sunday afternoon, Feb. 28th, 1943, saw a most successful presentation of an all-girl minstrel entitled
“Hi De Ho,” staged and directed by Mrs. M. J. Reiser. F ollowing the show supper was served by our Catering
Committee to all those present. Through the graciousne ss of our advertisers, we were able to publish our annual
program, which was a great financial success. The event was under the chairmanship of Mrs. Ben Chepenik and
Mrs. Morris Hammerman, assisted by a very capable committee.
Throughout the year, we contribute to the Jewish Braille Institute, Red Cross, Tuberculosis Association, Hope
Haven and to the USO.
By providing every Jewish child with the opportunity to receive a Jewish religious education, the Daughters of
Israel is an important factor in the preservation and spiritual growth of the local Jewish community.

JEWISH EDUCATION I
Since the decreased immigration of European Jews into this country
and with the growth of the increasingly complex pattern of social, eco¬
nomic, political and religious values, Jewish education has been flounder¬
ing between an education program based upon European traditions and
an educational program which reflects the American scene.
European education served its purpose admirably. With Jews living
a restricted Ghetto life, justly suspicious of the “liberalism” of Europe,
and voluntarily accepting Jewish life and its requirements, European Jew¬
ish education was not in conflict with the views and attitudes of the
greater number of people. But to take that program of Jewish education
and transplant it on American soil can result only in chaos and dissatisfac¬
tion, loss of interest and indifference. The American Jew does not live
a restricted Ghetto life, he is generally not suspicious of the social, political,
economic and religious changes, and he does not voluntarily accept Jewish
life with all of its requirements. Such a transplantation does not reflect
the views or attitudes of the greater numbers of the Jewish people, and
cannot but result in continuous conflict.
This over simplified analysis will become clearer if we pause for a
moment to consider the problem of religious services, Kashruth, Mikvah,
riding on the Sabbath, Ivri, and “Dovening,” tephlinin, and many other
problems with which congregations and individuals are concerned.
What shall be the objectives of the American Jewish educational pro¬
gram? The following objectives are suggestive, and motivated the educa¬
tion and recreation of the Center for the past year.
1. We must endeavor to inculcate in the Jew a love of his traditions,
a knowledge of his culture, a loyalty to his people and his community.
2. We must emphasize that there is no conflict between Americanism
and Judaism and that the Jew must always be prepared to defend and
even to fight for the social, economic, religious and political freedom for
all peoples in this country.
3. We must clarify the difference between those customs and prac¬
tices which resulted from the European Ghetto life and those which are
inherent in Judaism.
4. We must include in our Jewish educational program all such extra¬
curricular activities which will add interest, enjoyment, and dignity to the
Hebrew school.
The following pages will attempt to illustrate, although inadequately,
how the Center has attempted to put into practice some of the objectives
listed above. The extended educational program, and the activities in
which the child engaged are concrete expressions of the kind of program
that can be developed.

The past year the Center expanded
its educational program to include the
three-year-old child. It recognized the
well established fact that if we can con¬
dition and habituate the child to Jew¬
ish responses and acts, that that child
will be a better adjusted Jew when he
is older. To achieve this end, the Nur¬
sery Kindergarten was organized and
began to function on September 28th,
1942. This newest addition to the Cen¬
ter program has become one of the |
most successful Center undertakings. |
Under the thoroughly qualified direc-1
tion of Mrs. S. H. Rosenberg, who re-1
ceived her M. A. Degree in education |
and was licensed by New York State |
to direct and teach in the Nursery |
Kindergarten Schools, the school rapid-1
ly grew in numbers and in program. |
The children met daily, except Satur-j
day and Sunday, from 9 to 12 in the
morning. They received a full Nur¬
sery Kindergarten training, plus train¬
ing in Jewish acts and observances and
celebrations. I

School
i— 9J
At the beginning of this past year the Hebrew School presented a serious
problem. Due to a decrease in the teaching staff during the previous year,
the total number of registrants was only 55. The age groupings in the classes
covered 2-, 3- and 4-year spans, and the discipline in one class needed im¬
provement. The first step toward the correction of these problems was the
reorganization of the classes. This was not a difficult task, because the
students did not differ greatly as to what they had studied and covered.
The children were classified according to their ages into six divisions: 7 to 9
years, 9 to 10, 10 to 11, 11 to 12, 12 to 13, and a special beginners group from
11 to 13. The second step was the decision to use the Hasheiur Series, which
was introduced the previous year as the basis of curriculum. Since the
ultimate objective was to have a 7-year Hebrew course, a post Bar Mitzvah
group was also organized. Due to certain difficulties, however, this group
met together with the 12 to 13-year-old groups. In addition to the Hebrew
texts, the curriculum also included Sidur and prayers, customs and ceremonies,
assemblies and singing. The final step in this process of reorganization
involved a closer check on attendance, discipline, etc.
These measures were successful. At the end of June, the registration
of the school was 77, discipline was good, the classes were covering their
assigned work, and it was possible to divide the first age groups, the 7 to 9
year group, into two classes: 6 to 8 and 8 to 9. The children attending the
Bar Mitzvah preparation class were also required to attend regular Hebrew
classes and cover the prescribed work.

In June we were faced with the problem of a summer school, or rather
the extension of the Hebrew school to July 29. There were many reasons
why this was inadvisable. One of the important reasons was that in the
past about 35 children attended Hebrew School after the closing of the public
schools. However, it was decided to try the summer sessions again this year.
And contrary to all pessimistic expectations and despite the handicap of
working with two teachers in the place of three, at the time of the writing
of this report, July 20th, the school had a registration of more than 50
children and a very excellent attendance. The attendance tapered off to
about 25 at the end of July.

In conclusion, due to the excellent cooperation of our teachers, Mrs.
H. Abrams, Mr. J. Robin, Mr. S. H. Rosenberg and Rabbi M. D. Margolis,
the Talmud Torah progressed this past year. Mr. Rosenberg was the prin¬
cipal of the school as well as the teacher. To continue this progress it is
essential that the Center keep up the high requirements for the teaching
staff and that it continue with one carefully planned program for a number
of years. Thus will the Talmud Torah of the Center grow from strength
to strength.

Pledge recited by the Confirmands at the conclusion of the
Confirmation service:
I consecrate my life to my people and to my people’s faith. With all
my heart, with all my soul and with all my might will I endeavor to live
in the spirit of the lofty ideals of Judaism. I solemnly vow to help maintain
Jewish institutions, national, religious and charitable, to assist in the re¬
building of Eretz Yisroel as a national home for our oppressed brethren.
To live up to the highest ideals of American citizenship, to marry within
the Jewish faith and to live the life of a loyal Jew to the end of my days.
I shall ever be faithful to the belief in the oneness of God and the brother¬
hood of man. Sh’ma Yisroel, Adonoi Elohenu, Adonoi Echod. Hear, Oh
Israel the Lord Our God, the Lord is One.

REV. B. SAFER
Rev. B. Safer has catered to the religious needs of the
Jacksonville Jewish community for over forty years.
For many years he was the spiritual guide of congre¬
gation B’Nai Israel, serving in the capacity of Cantor,
Schochet and instructor of the Talmudic study group.
Rev. Safer has also served Jacksonville and neigh¬
boring Jewish communities in the capacity of Mohel.
For a number of years Rev. Safer served in the same
capacity in the Jewish community of Orlando. Today
he is the assistant cantor of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center, Ba’al Kriah and leader of the Ain Yaakov Class
which meets on Saturday afternoon in the Center.
During the High Holidays Rev. Safer conducts the
orthodox services in the auditorium and delivers talks
in Yiddish.
During his long career in Jacksonville he has acquired
many friends and is highly respected in the community.
We are indeed proud to have him in our midst and
grateful for his valuable services.
Recently Rev. Safer has been active in a movement
to build a pool for ritual purposes.
REV. JOSEPH ROBIN
This September Rev. Robin completes twelve years of
service with the Jacksonville Jewish Center. During
that time, Rev. Robin has not only become an integral
part of the organization, but has also endeared himself
to all of our members, young and old, and to the Jewish
community at large. Rev. Robin supervises the daily
“Minyon” and is responsible for the constant and un¬
interrupted function of these services. He also keeps a
correct record of Hebrew dates for the observance of
Yahrzeits for the deceased relatives of our members.
Rev. Robin also serves in the capacity of a teacher in
our Hebrew School, taking care of the Bar Mitzvah
classes. More than a hundred students have been pre¬
pared by him for Bar Mitzvah, all of them having been
imbued with a sense of reverence for our religion be¬
cause of his genial personality.
In addition there are a number of other odd tasks
which Rev. Robin performs, such as officiating on the
High Holy Days and reciting memorial prayers, both
in the Synagogue and on the cemetery at appropriate
times. Rev. Robin has also been in charge of the collec¬
tion of dues, a task which was made less difficult for
the Center because of the friendly relationship he has
established with the membership.
Rev. Robin has also been very active in Zionist work.
His interest centered chiefly in Jewish National Fund,
for which he has helped to collect large sums in Jack¬
sonville.

The crying need of the American 1
Jewish community seems to be adult |
education. While there is much organ-1
izational activity in American Jewish |
life there is very little appreciation and |
knowledge of the basic problems of |
Judaism and Israel. Every national or-1
| ganization has at one time or another |
| launched an educational campaign |
1 which generally met with failure. The |
j most noteworthy experiments which 1
| met with partial success are the Ha-1
jdassah Study Groups and the National |
[ Academy for Adult Jewish Studies |
| sponsored by the Jewish Theological 1
| Seminary. |
| For a number of years the Center |
| has been conducting evening classes |
| for adults in Hebrew, Jewish History |
| and Bible which were of varying dura-1
| tion and enjoyed some success. In 1938-1
139 a successful Round Table discussion |
j group met bi-weekly in the Rabbi’s |
| home. During the years 1940-42 Rabbi ¡
| Margolis led the Hadassah Study Group |
| in an intensive course in Zionism. ¡
| This year Mr. Rosenberg organized ¡
| two groups. One, a class in Bible, met ¡
| for about 20 sessions on Tuesday morn- ¡
| ings. The course dealt largely with cus-1
| toms and ceremonies and also touched I
| on many other problems. Another ¡
| group met for discussion on varying ¡
| subjects every Wednesday evening in f
| different homes. About twelve sessions [
| were held in all, each stimulating the f
| participants to a searching analysis of [
| the problems discussed. Towards the!
| end of the year Mr. Rosenberg met ¡
| with the Sunday school teachers for |
| about three sessions in an attempt to i
| deepen their understanding of the sub- |
| jects they were teaching. |
| Mention should also be made here ¡
| of the very successful experiment in |
| adult education sponsored by Senior ¡
j Hadassah under the chairmanship of i
| Mrs. D. H. Lasarow during this past |
| year. A group of from twenty-five to |
j fifty women met once a month at the ¡
| Seminole hotel at both a morning and ¡
| afternoon session. Between the two ses-1
| sions a luncheon was served which was ¡
| addressed by a communal leader or vis-1
| iting speaker on an important phase ¡
1 of Jewish life. This was a very success-1
| ful venture, credit for which should go ¡
| largely to the resourcefulness and zeal |
| of our energetic Mrs. Lazarow.
j It is hoped that during the coming ¡
| year more members of our congrega- ¡
| tion will exhibit a desire for increasing |
| their knowledge of Judaism and will
| make possible the formation of new
| adult education groups.

Jacksonville Jewish
Center Sunday School
The Sunday School during the past
year was under the direction of Mr.
Samuel H. Rosenberg. The curriculum
as in the past, provided for a kinder¬
garten and a ten year course in History
and Customs and Ceremonies. The So-
loff series of history books, which were
hitherto used in grades five, six and
seven were retained but each book
spread over a two-year period, begin¬
ning with the fourth grade. The third
grade used Hillel’s Happy Holidays in¬
stead of Ish Kishor’s History of Israel.
The registration in all classes num¬
bered about 225. Attendance of both
teachers and pupils was good. Regular
assemblies twice a month were held in
the auditorium, with members of the
different classes preparing the pro¬
grams. Efforts were also made during
the year to build up in the children
interest in home ceremonies, such as
lighting candles and reciting the Kid-
dush as well as attendance at Syna¬
gogue services.
The staff of the Sunday School con¬
sisted of the following: Mrs. M. J. Rei¬
ser, Mrs. Samuel H. Rosenberg, Miss
Victoria Levy, Mrs. Philip Selber, Mrs.
Harry Abrams, Miss Raela Fink, Mias
Eunice Davis, Miss Mollye Mizrahi, Mr.
Morris Ansbacher, Mr. M. J. Reiser
and Mr. Philip Selber. Rabbi M. D.
Margolis met with the Confirmation
Class on Wednesday afternoons for a
course in Bible studies and the prepara¬
tion for the Confirmation ceremony.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center owes
a debt of gratitude to the fine staff of
teachers whose loyalty and devotion
to Jewish education has made possible
the development and upbuilding of our
fine Sunday School.

THE
JUNIOR
CONGREGATION
During the past year a new experi¬
ment was tried with the Junior Con¬
gregation. Instead of meeting sepa¬
rately in the Prayer Room on Sabbath
mornings, the Junior Congregation met
in the main Synagogue, joining the
older worshippers at 9:45 for the read¬
ing of the Torah and staying through
the end of Musaf. The attendance va¬
ried from about 15 to 35 children. While
the pupils of the Hebrew School who
attended gained a first hand experience
with the procedure and ritual of the
adult Synagogue they were not able
to participate with understanding in
the prayers themselves and they lost
their identity as a Junior Congrega¬
tion. No officers are therefore listed
for the year. It is hoped that at the
beginning of the coming year a new
plan may be adopted which may re¬
tain the advantages of both the new
and the old arrangements.

Rabbi M. D. Margolis
During the five years that Rabbi Margolis has been conducting the Center Radio Program
over station WJAX, he has received many letter s of commendation, including requests for copies
of the program. These requests could not often b e granted because of the cost involved. During
the past year especially the radio program was given every Sunday afternoon and the cost of
mimeographing would be prohibitive. To satisf y the desire of many of our members and friends
for copies of these talks, we are reprinting herewith the Rabbi’s radio talk entitled “The Evil
Tongue,” which made a deep impression on many listeners and which is still timely.
THE EVIL TONGUE — (Radio address delivered over WJAX at 12:15 on April 4, 1943.)
Of all the diseases that ever afflicted mankind the most dreaded disease was leprosy. In Bible times it was a
widespread affliction, and the priests of Israel were instructed how to curb the spread of the disease. The leper was tc
wear special garments, his head uncovered, but his face hi dden in his mantle over his mouth. He was to live outside
the walls of the city in complete isolation. Should someone come near him the leper was to warn the approaching
person by saying “Unclean, unclean.”
The rabbis of the Talmud found the counterpart of this disease of the body in the moral realm. By analysing
the Hebrew word “Metzora,” meaning leper, they found that it could be interpreted to mean “He who invents evil
reports or “slander.” They came to the conclusion that just as leprosy is the most terrible disease of the body, so
slander is the most terrible disease of the soul, causing untold misery and suffering to humanity.
Just as leprosy, they pointed out, begins with an innocent looking white spot on the skin and spreads imper-
ceptably over the whole body, so does the evil tongue, slander, often begin with a little vile remark innocently
uttered, which eventually may bring great harm to society as a whole, spreading hatred between man and his
brother, between husband and wife, between one religion and another and between one people and another.
One Jewish sage compares the power of slander to that of a swiftly flying arrow. Just as an arrow, he says,
once it darts forth cannot be taken back, so slander once spoken cannot be taken back but continues its evil mis¬
sion. Another sage explains why slander is sometimes called “The Triple Tongue.” Because, says he, it demoralizes
and often kills all three—the person who first utters it, the person who believes it and spreads it and the person who
is being slandered.
All this, my friends, was not idle speculation. The Jewish sages of the Talmud spoke from bitter experience.
They knew at first hand of the suffering of our people brought about by the power of the evil tongue, by slander,
false accusation and libel. In the Middle Ages, during the bubonic plague which ravaged half of the population
of Europe, Jews were accused of poisoning the wells. Instead of concentrating on fighting the plague, the people
of Europe attacked the innocent Jews. All suffered alike. The slanderers, the slandered and those who believed
the evil accusation.
The rise of Hitlerism is the best example of the power of the evil tongue. After the first World War when Ger¬
many was disillusioned and heartsick from defeat, Hitler a psycopathic, hate-blinded corporal, was involved in a
street brawl. As the crowd gathered about him he rose from the ground and began to address the people. Since
the idea of defeat was uppermost in the mind of everybody, he uttered the opinion that Germany was not defeated
but betrayed by the Jews. The accusation, hatched in the inflamed and feverish brain of a maniac, caught on like
wild fire. Jew hatred became the very cornerstone of the Nazi idealogy. It became a poisonous weapon with which
to deaden the sensibilities of Germany’s intended victims of aggression.
The result was tragic indeed. It became a triple-edged sword. It killed millions of Germans who believed this
slander. It killed millions of Jews who were the objects of this accusation and it will ultimately bring the de¬
struction of him who invented it.
The struggle is not over yet. Hitler knows that he is doomed on the battlefields, and therefore, to save him¬
self, is using the old weapon of slander by which he hopes to divide his enemies, to bring the dissension into their
ranks and soften them for defeat. Again the Jew is the main target of this propaganda. A few months ago the Ber¬
lin radio in the course of an English broadcast intended for America, sent over the ether a little jingle which begins
by lauding the bravery of Captain Colin Kelly who sank a Jap battleship and ends with a slanderous jibe imputing
the patriotism of the American Jew. This bit of malicious propaganda is passed on in varying form by Hitler’s
stooges in this country in many imperceptible ways. As a humorous verse injected into an after-dinner speech,
as a slip of paper passed from hand to hand to men in uniform and in many other ways. It’s harmless, isn’t it?
It’s an innocent joke, but this is how leprosy begins, according to the Bible, as an innocent white spot on the skin.
There is no use defending one’s self against this propaganda. Those who know the facts and figures are well
aware of the truth that Jews are contributing more than their share in men and material support to the prosecu¬
tion of the war. Thousands of Jewish young men are fighting in Africa and in the South Pacific in every branch
of the United States service. Many of them have given their lives. Many have been decorated for bravery. Meyer
Levin, the boy from Brooklyn, was the bombardier on the Flying Fortress piloted by Captain Colin Kelly of Madi¬
son, Florida, which sank the Jap battleship Haruma at the beginning of the war. Meyer Levin survived the crash
of the plane and fought on for many months to avenge the death of his heroic friend, Captain Kelly, until he too
died in defense of his country.
Commenting on the death of Meyer Levin, the New York Herald Tribune, in an editorial dedicated to the memo¬
ry of the two fliers says, “Together those two names rebuke the little sneers, the mean prejudices, the narrow fears
that froth and bubble on the surface of the American melting pot. Together they bespeak that brotherhood which
is certainly not the least of the stakes of this war. War has shame of its own, and snobberies, but qne cannot
believe they survive long in the thin, dry upper air, where the lives of a bomber’s crew hang upon the naked skill,
courage and steadfastness of each man—whatever his name, whatever his birthplace, whatever the manner in which
he calls upon his God. The earthbound need this cleansing knowledge, therefore, it will be well to keep green the
memory of Captain Colin Kelly of Madison, Fla., and of Sergeant Meyer Levin, Brooklyn. And I should like to add,
that if you, as a patriotic American, wish to help Americ a win the victory, keep green in your mind the Biblical
lesson regarding lepers and steer clear of and isolate the moral lepers in our midst who are helping the enemy
by spreading his venemous and morale-shattering propa ganda. You can render no greater service to your country.
It is related in the Talmud that Rabbi Yanai once heard a peddler shouting his wares in the street in the follow¬
ing manner: “Buy the elixir of life. Sold very cheaply.” Thinking that the man was selling fake medicine the ven¬
erable rabbi went out to test his claim. But when he offe red to buy the elixir of life, the peddler produced a book
of Psalms and pointed to the verse: “What man is who desireth life? Keep thy tongue from evil and thy lips from
speaking guile.” This is a lesson which we all have to learn. When we do it will usher in an era of peace, brother¬
hood and happiness for all mankind.

Melvin Margol Advisor
Mendy Margol Junior Advisor
Betty Weinstein A.Z.A. Sweetheart
Outstanding among local Jewish groups
is the Junior Order of B’nai B’rith. Led by
their energetic and untiring advisor, Melvin
Margol, together with the advisory board
and a group of capable officers, the local
chapter has once more risen to attain great
heights in carrying out the ideals of the
national “Five Fold and Full” plan. To fill
the gaps in membership caused by the loss
of a large number of older fellows to the
armed services, the Supreme Advisory Coun¬
cil lowered the age limit to 14. The local
chapter took full advantage of this op¬
portunity by immediately bringing in these
younger boys to fill in the ranks of those
boys who are now serving in the armed
services of our country.
The outstanding event of the year was
the annual Valentine dance given February
13. The dance was held in the beautifully
decorated auditorium of the Jax Jewish
Center. It was attended by a large group
of young people, among which were many
college boys. The feature of the evening
was the presentation of A. Z. A. sweetheart,
Miss Rita Gendzier, with a large bouquet
of beautiful American roses. The retiring-
sweetheart was given an official sweetheart
pin and guard. A beautiful wallet was
presented to Melvin Margol in appreciation
for his services for the past year. The Sec¬
retary, Leonard Silver, was awarded a check
for his untiring efforts in making the dance
a financial as well as social success. Music
was furnished by the popular band of Miss
Addie Berry.
The chapter also handled all arrange¬
ments for the' Georgia-Florida dance, which
was sponsored by the A. Z. A. and the
Hillel Foundation of the Florida University
jointly.
The chapter also sponsored a Friday
Night Service during the1 month of March.
The chapter assisted in the annual J. N. F.
drive under the supervision of Mr. J. Robin.
The chapter received national recognition
for its efforts in the Russian War Relief
drive. The picture of the advisors and the
chairman of the drive appeared in the
national magazine, The Shofar.
The past year also found the local A. Z.
A. very successful in atheletics, under the
coaching of its junior advisor, Melvin
Margol. The A. Z. A. captured the “Y”
basketball league. It also defeated the
Esquire Club 5 to 3 and 4 to 3 in soft ball
while dropping a 13 to 7 decision in foot¬
ball.
To Leonard Silver and Beryl Weinstein
much credit is due for the successful affairs
of the chapter, especially the Valentine
dance and minstrel. At the present time
the boys are working on the fourth annual
minstrel and fashion show, scheduled to be
held the end of August and which will
culminate a most successful year of A. Z.
A. activities.
The chapter is also preparing to send a
basketball team, a debating team and an
orator to the forthcoming Florida State
A. Z. A. convention to be held over the
Labor Day week-end.

An opportunity for adventure
and achievement is open to boys
of nine, ten and eleven years of
age through the fine program of
Cubbing. It is a colorful activity
that is quite natural to boys of this
age and leads to the gateway of
Scouting where they begin a new
and different kind of adventure.
Cubbing gives the parents the
finest opportunity for joint partici¬
pation with the child, as Mother,
Dad and the home are all tightly
bound into the Cubbing program.
During the past three years Cub
Pack 14 enjoyed considerable suc¬
cess. However, last vear because
of the preoccupation with war ac¬
tivities and shortage in help, there
was a decrease in the activities of
the Pack. Parents found it dif¬
ficult to attend meetings and to
cooperate in Pack projects. It is
hoped that during the coming year
the Pack will be able to launch a
full program of activities.

During the past year the girls of
Troop 2, sponsored by the Daugh¬
ters of Israel and led by Mrs. Louis
Slott and Mrs. Sinclair Afchan
have made much progress.
The majority of girls have com¬
pleted their second class require¬
ments and are well on their way
toward first class.
They have contributed gen¬
erously to the Girl Scout Victory
Fund and have collected maga¬
zines and books for use of service
men.
Because of their outstanding
achievements, Sylvia Flauman and
Eileen Grossman were chosen to
represent Troop 2 at a city-wide
week end at Camp Chowenwah.
While there the girls learned the
democratic way of scouting in
work as well as play.
Girl Scouting offers numerous
outlets for the enthusiasm of voung
girls from ten to fourteen. With
ten program fields to choose from,
including first aid, arts and crafts,
music and dancing, out-of-door
sports and games, nature, com¬
munity life, homemaking, etc.,
every girl should be able to find
that for which she is best suited.
The members of the troop have
a warm welcome for all interested
in scouting.

During the preceding year the
Senior Daughters of Judea organized
a club for the girls who were too
young to join the seniors. They called
this club the Junior Daughters of
Judea, which they sponsored until the
Junior Hadassah appointed for them
a capable leader, Now it is one of the
more active clubs in Jacksonville.
The Senior club sponsored a hot dog
supper and a club party during the
year. They also participated in Young
Judean Sabbath, Mother’s Day ser¬
vices and similar affairs.

H. C. AVERY, Pres.
During the coming year the club
hopes that Junior Hadassah will ap¬
point for them a capable leader so that
they may be able to learn more about
Palestinian customs and Palestinian
songs and dances. The club looks for¬
ward to an enlarged program during
the coming year, which will include
handicrafts and more social affairs.

This group was organized last
year by Mrs. M. J. Reiser and met
weekly under her leadership.
Much of the time was spent in
getting acquainted with Young
Judea purposes and ideals and be¬
coming organized. One project
which was enjoyed was rolling
bandages for the Red Cross. The
boys and girls had a number of
interesting social events. The club
plans to have a more intensive pro¬
gram next year. Among the proj¬
ects contemplated are the showing
of pictures of life in Palestine and
raising of more money for National
Young Judea and other Palestine
projects.

The Junior Daughters of Judea
is composed of girls in the 9th and
10th grades in Public School.
During the past year the girls had
a successful raffle to raise money
for the Karen Hanoar Fund. In
March the club sponsored a dance
in the Center Auditorium for all
the other Jewish youth clubs in
Jacksonville.
At the meetings some of the
great Jewish men and women of
the past were discussed and all
members of the club participated.
At the end of the year a quiz was
held to see how much each girl
knew about them.
The Closing affair was an all-day
trip to the Beach which was
thoroughly enjoyed by all attend¬
ing.

Eunice Davis,
To uphold and continue the name
the Henrietta Szold Young Judean
Club has made foritself in this city,
this organization, through its many
American and Zionist activities has
made remarkable progress during the
past year.
Starting the winter season off a
“pot-luck” supper was held at the
home of Miss Raela Fink, co-sponsor
with Miss Eunice Davis.
The membership of the organization
has increased constantly, taking girls
from the ages of 13 to 16, and
especially those new to the Jewish
Community. The total number of
members has reached 30.

In helping to contribute to the
Keren Hanoar Fund in Palestine,
which Young Judaea supports the club
participated in several activities, such
as dances, rafffes and candy sales. The
majority of the members cooperated
in the J. N. F. blue box collections,
flower day sales, and Mother’s Day cer¬
tificates sales.
Through the cultural programs di¬
rected by Sarah Dwoskin and her com¬
mittee, the members were able to gain
an appreciation of Jewish customs and
ceremonies, current events and to en¬
joy Palestine folk songs and dances.
The music was directed by Marion
Freed. At the Young Judean Sabbath
held in the Center Synagogue, a paper
was read by Rita Gendzier on what
“Young Judaea Means to Me,” a co¬
operative project of the club. A num¬
ber of the members attended the
Leadership Training Course given by
Dr. Aharon Kessler of National Young
Judaea under the auspices of the local
Zionist Youth Commission. Twenty-
nine girls enjoyed a four-day week¬
end house party at the Jacksonville
Beach home of Mrs. Joe Becker, which
was the culminating event of the sea¬
son. A “rush” party was also held on
July 29 at the home of Sarah Dwoskin
for prospéctive members, featuring the
playlet, “Thumbs Up for Palestine.”

BELOW IS A LIST OF THE USUAL EXCUSES GIVEN BY PARENTS FOR NOT SENDING THEIR
1 CHILDREN TO HEBREW SCHOOL. DO YOU KNOW THE ANSWERS?
Excuse 1: “Child is too busy.”
Answer: Time must be found for the child's Jew¬
ish education, for it is of supreme importance to
the future happiness of the child, his family and
his community. How about the tens of thousands
of Jewish children who are enrolled in Hebrew
schools? They manage to find time. Certainly the
child's Jewish education is more important than in¬
struction in music and other special fields. The
class time of the child can be adjusted so as not
to conflict with his other activities.
Excuse 2: “Child is too young.”
Answer: A thorough Jewish education requires
at least six or seven years of continuous study. The
child's Jewish education should begin when his
Public School education begins. The young child
is highly impressionistic and it is important that he
be under the influence of a Hebrew school during
his early years. There are thousands of children
between the ages of six and nine in Jewish schools.
The classes for younger children meet immediately
after Public School so that the children may be
home early. The program of instruction for
younger children includes songs, stories, games
and other activities which make their learning
pleasant and interesting.
Excuse 3: Girls do not need Jewish education.”
Answer: These are modern times, when girls re¬
ceive as good a general education as do boys. Our
girls should be treated as the equals of boys also in
their Jewish education. The Jewish girl of today is
the Jewish mother of tomorrow. Upon her will de¬
pend the kind of Jewish homes we are to have in
the future. Hers will be the responsibility of bring¬
ing up the next generation. She must be adequate¬
ly prepared for these tasks. Tens of thousands of
Jewish girls are enrolled in Hebrew schools and
are benefitting greatly from this Jewish education.
Do not deprive your girl of her due.
Excuse 4: There is plenty of time to prepare the
child for Bar Mitzvah.”
Answer: If Bar Mitzvah is to mean something to
the boy, he should study in a good school for sev¬
eral years. At least two to three years are necessary
to give the boy a minimum preparation for Bar
Mitzvah.
Excuse 5: “Child is studying privately.”
Answer: A well-organized school is the best
place for the child. The school has a systematic
course of study, which is taught by trained teach¬
ers and is supervised by a principal. Private in¬
struction is usually inefficient and unsatisfactory.
In a school, the child makes better progress be¬
cause he is studying with other children, and is
stimulated by them. The holiday celebrations, re¬
ligious services, singing and other activities make
the learning interesting and enjoyable. He not only
acquires Jewish knowledge but also learns how to
live as a Jew. The child needs the social milieu
which the school provides.
Excuse 6: “None of the child's playmates go to
Hebrew School.”
Answer: Child will acquire new friends in the
Hebrew school and thus widen his circle of play¬
mates and acquaintances.
Excuse 7: “Child is attending Sunday School.”
Answer: It is impossible to obtain a satisfactory
Jewish education by attending only once a week.
There is so much of Jewish history, religion, He¬
brew and other subjects to be studied. Many of the
better Sunday schools are now beginning to or¬
ganize week-day classes for their pupils.
Excuse 8: “Other children dropped out.”
Answer: Very often pupils drop out because
their attendance is poor and therefore they make
no progress. Children who attend regularly and do
their work well usually continue until they are
graduated.
Excuse 9: “Tuition rates are too high.”
Answer: There is no cost for the Jewish educa¬
tion that your child will receive, in our Talmud
Torah. Tuition is free for children of our members
and to children whose parents cannot afford to be
members of our Center.
REGISTER YOUR CHILD IN THE HEBREW
SCHOOL TODAY!

JEWS IN AMERICAN WARS
(Ed. Note: Today the insidious influence of Nazi prop¬
aganda often leads well-meaning but misguided individuals
to question the patriotism of the Jew. The Jew has every
reason to be proud of his participation in the various Amer¬
ican wars for his is indeed a glorious history in this respect,
as will be seen from the following account presented through
the courtesy of the Anti-Defamation Teague of B’nai
B’rith.)
THE REVOLUTIONARY WAR
Though the records of the Continental Army are in¬
complete, there is ample evidence to show that the less
than 3,000 Jews in the United States during the colonial
period contributed generously to the cause of the Revo¬
lution.
There was a high proportion of officers among the
Jews in the Revolutionary Army. The records of twenty-
seven are in the archives.
Haym Salomon—Patriot from Poland
More than any other Jewish person in the colonies,
Haym Salomon, who came to Philadelphia from Lissa,
Poland, in 1772, gave of his energy and substance for
the cause of freedom in America. Arrested and impris¬
oned as a sympathizer with the Revolution when New
York was captured by the British, Salomon first helped
American and French prisoners to escape and then fled
himself, going to Philadelphia, the mecca of patriots.
In Philadelphia he began his never-ending efforts to
secure funds for the ragged armies of George Washing¬
ton. There was no limit to his zeal. Even the holiest day
of the Jewish religion, Yom Kippur, found Salomon in¬
terrupting the awesome service to raise $400,000.00 from
the congregants at the behest of General Washington.
Salomon gave $200,000.00 of his own funds to the
cause of the American Revolution. When he died in
1785, he left his wife and four little children penniless.
The poverty-stricken family was never reimbursed by
the Government.
THE WAR OF 1812
As in the Revolutionary W ar, Jews were prominent
as officers. Some of the most outstanding were Brig.
Gen. Joseph Bloomfield; Col. Nathan Meyers; Captains
Myer Moses, Mordecai Myers, and Levi C. Harby. In the
Navy, Uriah P. Levy reached the highest rank. Captain
John Ordroneaux with other American Privateers
played an important part in the successful conclusion
of the struggle by preying on British commerce on the
high seas. A number of Jews enlisted in the home de¬
fense forces of Baltimore when that city was attacked
by the enemy.
THE WAR WITH MEXICO
General David de Leon, twice cited for gallantry by
Congress, was the outstanding Jew in the military
forces. Other Jewish officers recorded include: Sur¬
geon General Moses Albert Levy, who served in Gen¬
eral Sam Houston’s army; David S. Kauffman, who was
Aide to General Douglas and Lieutenant Henry Seelig-
son, who received special commendation for bravery
from General Taylor. A proportionately significant
number of Jews fought with the American Army.
THE CIVIL WAR PERIOD
When the outbreak of the Civil War divided the
American people on the issues of the crisis, it also
divided the sentiments of the Jewish citizens. Those
who lived in the South were loyal to the cause of the
Confederacy. Some of the outstanding pro-slavery
spokesmen came from this group. David Yulee (Levy),
Senator from the State of Florida, and Judah P. Ben¬
jamin, Senator from Louisiana, are probably the best
known. Later, Yulee was elected Governor of his state.
Benjamin was first Secretary of War and then Secre¬
tary of State under Jefferson Davis, President of the
Confederate States.
Twelve hundred Jews fought in the armies of the
Confederacy and more than 6300 served with the Union
forces. The highest ranking Jewish officer in the North¬
ern army was Brigadier General Frederick Knefler,
an immigrant from Hungary. General David de Leon of
Mexican War fame achieved the highest ranking among
the Jewish men in the Southern army.
Three hundred and thirty-six Jewish soldiers are
known to have been kiled in action; three hundred six¬
teen were wounded. This represented a casualty .rate
of more than than eight per cent of the total enlisted
Jewish personnel.
THE SPANISH-AMERICAN WAR
More than four thousand Jewish men volunteered for
service in the Spanish-American War. There were many
more Jews, hundreds of whom were members of the
regular army and navy, who participated in the strug¬
gle. Fifteen Jews were among the crew of the Maine
whose sinking in Havana precipitated America’s entry
into the war. Sergeant Maurice Juster of the First Cali¬
fornia Volunteers was the first soldier to fall in the
battle of Manila.
In the Army, thirteen Jews were officers; in the Navy,
twenty w ore officers’ stripes. Lieutenant Joseph Strauss
in the Spanish-American War rose to Rear Admiral in
the World War and Commander of Asiatic Fleet in
1921-22. Edward David Taussig, who was a Lieut. Com¬
mander in 1898, became a Rear Admiral in 1909.
WORLD WAR I
When America entered the World War, the proportion
of Jews in the general population amounted to three
per cent. Enrollment of Jews in the army and navy was
between four and five per cent. The most important
reason for this can be found in the large number of
Jewish volunteers. Approximately 40,000 Jewish men
volunteered, accounting for 18% of the entire Jewish
enrollment.
A study made by Julian Leavitt, Director of the
Board of Jewish War Records, reveals that 225,000 Jews
served in the various branches of the armed forces of
the United States during the World War. Of these 171,-
000 were in the Army; 23,500 in the Navy; 12,250 in the
Marine Corps; and 18,000 in other branches of the
service.
There were nearly 10,000 commissioned officers in
the various branches of the service. Of these, 1,000 were
i nthe Navy.
There were 1,100 citations for valor conferred on the
Jewish men in the American Army. Of these 723 were
conferred by the American Command and 366 by the
Allied Commands. The Congressional Medal of Honor,
the highest privilege in the nation, conferred in all on
but ninety men in the entire war, was awarded to six
Jews. The Distinguished Service Cross went to 150, the
French Medaille Militaire was won by four American
Jews and the Croix de Guerre by one hundred seventy-
five Jews. The instances of individual heroism are too
many to permit enumeration. Suffice it to say that the
exploits of Abraham Krotoshinsky, who saved the fa¬
mous Lost Battalion; the bravery of Albert Cohen, the
youngest American soldier of the war who enlisted at
the age of thirteen and who was killed in the Meuse-
Argonne offensive; and the heroism of Sam Dreben,
“the fighting Jew/’ typify the courage and spirt of the
American Jewish soldier.
WORLD WAR II
Space does not permit more than a brief recital of the
role of the Jews, in World War II. From the very out¬
break of the war at Pearl Harbor there were a score
of Jewish heroes, and Jews have distinguished themsel¬
ves ever since on all fronts. There is, for example, Com¬
mander Solomon Isquith of Brooklyn, of the target ship
Utah, who was decorated with the Navy Cross for res¬
cuing 90% of the crew of that vessel. There is the im¬
mortal Meyer Levin, Colin Kelly’s bombardier. There is
Chaplain Goode who died arm in arm with a Protestant
and Catholic chaplain after all three gave their life
belts to others on a sinking ship.
It is too early yet for accurate statistics on the exact
number of Jewish men in service. Some time ago it was
estimated as being over 300,000. So far as our own
community is concerned (and it is a fair basis for judg¬
ing), between eight and ten per cent of our boys are
in service, while our percentage in the general popula¬
tion of Jacksonville is at best no more than 2 to 3
per cent.
Perhaps the Jew’s part in World War II can best be
summarized in the words of a great fighting man, Brig.
Gen. Frank T. Hines, who said:
“From the Revolutionary War, on down through all
of our other wars, members of the Jewish faith have
amply demonstrated their love of country, and their
willingness to sacrifice, and if need be to die, to preserve
the freedoms which are our heritage, and the corner¬
stone of our national life.
“Americans of your faith have a fighting tradition
which they are now gallantly upholding.
Many of their daring accomplishments have already
been recorded. In no war in our history have they
fought more bravely nor more tenaciously than in the
present bitter conflict. All Americans honor them for
their devotion.”

Under the auspices of the Daughters of Israel the Center has
made a fine contribution to the war effort. During the winter months
members of the organization met regularly once or twice a week,
as the need arose to sew for the Red Cross. The women completed
702 garments, including pajamas, kit bags, boys flannel shirts, slips,
men s shirts, petticoats and blouses. This work was under the chair¬
manship of Mrs. Charles Fink and Mrs. Cele Schemer.
Mrs. Abe Shapiro led an enthusiastic group of women through-
r?'
out the summer twice a week in Red Cross bandage work, on Mon¬
days from 7 P. M. to 10 P. M. and on Wednesdays from 9 A. M. to 4
P. M. The auditorium of the Jacksonville Jewish Center was utilized
as the work room. Under the supervision of Mrs. Shapiro the wom¬
en, among whom were also Gentile neighbors, accomplished a great
deal of indispensable work. Several times during the season the Red
Cross commended the Jewish Center group for their fine accom¬
plishments, having exceeded several times their quota and having
produced more than any other group in the city.
Mrs. Shapiro was ably assisted by Mrs. David Moscovitz, chief
Instructor; Mrs. Sam Bryan, chairman of supplies; and Mrs. E. L.
Henry, chairman of packing. Richard Talisman, boy scout of Troop
14, assisted the group on various occasions.

Six years ago the Center Cemetery was re-purchased
§ from the Evergreen Cemetery Association and since that
| time its grounds have been steadily improved and beauti-
| fied and a definite policy as to the type of monuments
a instituted.
The committee is now composed of ten members, all
| of whom serve for a period of five years and are elected
| by the Board of Directors. It maintains its own treasury
1 and supervises all expenditures and assessments in con-
| nection with the Cemetery.
An auxiliary committee, known as the Shamoshim Com-
fj mittee, assists the Chevra Kadisha in performing all
| burials according to the orthodox ritual. The Shamoshim
| committee is headed by Rev. Joseph Robin.
| Since the death of Mr. Simon Selber, Mr. Oscar Magazis
| has been overseeing the field work at the Cemetery.

THE CENTER AND THE
COMMUNITY
The following pages are dedicated
to a description of the various Jewish
organizations in the community. These
organizations are autonimous inde¬
pendent bodies. Yet the Center has
directly and indirectly had a pro¬
found influence upon their work and
has contributed greatly to their pro¬
grams.
The great majority of the organiza¬
tions use the Center as a meeting place.
The Zionist groups receive idealogical
and moral support from the Center
pulpit, Hebrew School and Sunday
School. The Rabbi and the Teachers
of the Center are often called upon
to lead study groups and help plan the
cultural programs of these organiza¬
tions. The Jewish Community Council
summons its leaders and workers from
among the ranks of the members of the
Jewish Center. The Rabbi and the
Vice-President served as Executive
Secretaries of the Community Council
for the past six years. The Center
President and members of its Board
are among the most active leaders of
the Community Council. The same
may be said about many of the other
organizations, the Y. M. H. A., the
B’Nai B’Rith, the Jewish Welfare
Board, etc.
The Center Synagogue serves the
religious needs of nearly 400 member
families and many unaffiliated fami¬
lies. It maintains the only “Minyan”,
morning and evening, in the City.
Both the orthodox and the conserva¬
tive rituals are adhered to on the High
Holy Days so that every Jew who
wishes to worship in the Center may
find complete satisfaction.
The Center maintains a Talmud
Torah in the Community which is open
to all, members and non-members.
This year two buses will provide
transportation to Talmud Torah pupils
from many sections of the City.
The Center sponsors a Boy Scout and
Girl Scout troop and a Cub Pack. It
assists in every possible way the Young
Judean Clubs and the A. Z. A. fraterni¬
ty.
The Center conducts a weekly radio
program over station WJAX which
features brief talks on Judaism and re¬
lated subjects and which enjoys a wide
audience both among Jews and Gen¬
tiles.
Thus the Center is carrying out the
purpose for which it was originally
brought into being . It is the hub of the
wheel of Community life. It radiates
its inspiration for Jewish living and
provides the majority of our Jewish
men, women and children with re¬
ligious, educational and social activities
throughout the year.

There is no doubt in the mind and heart of any sincere Jew that Zionism
is one of the most urgent problems facing the Jews of America. The peace
that will follow the present war must take cognizance of the place of the
Jew in the post-war world. It is only through the efforts of a strong, united
body of Jews that the peace-makers will recognize and grant the justice of
the Jew’s claim to Palestine.
The Jacksonville Zionist District, under the efficient and inspiring leader¬
ship of its president, Mr. Harry Katz, has fully recognized its responsibility
and the part it must play in the struggle for Palestine. The work of the past
year is indicative of the contributions made by the local Zionist District to
world Zionism.
In cooperation with other Zionist districts, the local district conducted a
war bond drive under the joint chairmanship of Mr. Samuel H. Rosenberg
and Mr. S. D. Kramer. More than $75,000.00 was subscribed by Jacksonville
through the efforts of the Zionist district.
The local district obtained a resolution, voted upon and passed by the
Florida Legislature, endorsing Palestine as the Jewish National Homeland.
The program committee, under the chairmanship of Rabbi M. D. Margolis,
arranged interesting and provocative meetings throughout the year. One of
the finest meetings arranged by this committee was the lecture by Dr. Ludwig
Lewisohn. Dr. Lewisohn spoke in the Jacksonville Jewish Center auditorium
where 400 people had gathered to listen to his inspiring message.
Mr. Harry Katz was instrumental in the formation of an American Pales¬
tine Committee composed of the outstanding non-Jewish leaders in Jack¬
sonville.
The Youth Commission, under the co-chairmanship of S. H. Rosenberg
and Mrs. Irving Brukner, received the full support of the Zionist and Ha-
dassah membership. This commission concerned itself with the problems
of Junior Hadassah, Young Judean Clubs, leaders, programming, and activi¬
ties. They also sponsored an unusually fine institute conducted by Dr. Aharon
Kessler.
The finest evidence of the growth of community interest in Zionism
can be seen in the tremendously increased membership of the local district.
The membership committee, under the co-chairmanship of Rabbi M. D. Mar¬
golis and Rev. Joseph Robin, conducted an intensive campaign which re¬
sulted in an increase of the membership of the Zionist District from 123
members to 263—an increase of 140 members over the past year! This is
truly a phenomenal achievement. The district is planning an intensive cul¬
tural program for the coming year. We know that the membership will be
increased by even greater numbers than this year.
