Hadasah is the, Women’s Zionist Organization of America whose
work has been invaluable in maintaining Palestine as a national
Jewish homeland. The marvelous accomplishments there in the
past 30 years have been greatly due to this group of women.
Hadassah has undertaken the task of making Palestine a country
both habitable and healthful, a work which ranks only second
to the heroic work of the Jewish pioneers themselves. Through
the establishment of a network of medical clinics, hospitaliza¬
tion nursing’ service and modern methods of sanitation, Hadassaah
has practically abolished the dread malaria and trachoma.
Hadassah’s Medical Organization is responsible for a great
~ program of preventive and curative medicine in Palestine. The
= facilities developed there in the last 3 0 years have been turned
= over to the Allies and Palestine is now doing its share to defeat
= the enemy. For many months these facilities were placed at
I the disposal of American troops who Were arriving secretly to take
= part in the crucial battle of the Aliddle East,
a
So vital is our work that our government has given us marked
a cooperation in that the many supplies which we send to the hos-
i pitáis and institutions in Palestine, are given priority. On many
= occasions orders of drugs which are ready for shipment in the
= morning are cleared by afternoon and by night are aboard planes
a en route to the Middle East. Through its Donors Affair and the
= Annual Fall Dance, the Jacksonville chapter contributes its share
| toward the Hadassah Medical Organization Fund.
|
The Child Welfare Service of Hadassah, which is non-sectarian,
= reaches 25,000 boys and girls through.- the school luncheon pro-
13 gram, which provides lessons in nutrition, cooking a meal, plan-
= ning, and serves free meals to the underprivileged. Its medical
= supervision and school hygiene program reaches 75,000 children.
= Through the Thrift Luncheons here, the local chapter raises its
3 quota for this fund.
Through our project of purchasing and redeeming land in
Palestine, we make it possible to increase the production of vital
foods, to continue the all-out program of war production. The
land is also our investment for the future, when it is hoped
the country will become one of the chief answers to the problem
of homelessness now facing hundreds of thousands of our
brethren in Europe and other lands.
Through our Youth Aliyah movement, we have been able to
save and rehabilitate 8,500 refugee children during the past nine
years. Just recently Palestine received 1,098 children who have
undergone many hardships. One of the indications of the suffering
they had experienced was the fact that most of their teeth had
been ruined because of the ravages of hunger and the precarious
living they had undergone. Of course the children were given
the* best of medical attention and most of them are going into
the various agricultural settlements, but the younger ones will
be adopted. by individuals or educated in institutions. It is our
hope to bring into Palestine at least 25,000 refugee children in
the immediate future.
In local war activities many of our members are participating
in the various phases of war work—Red Cross work, serving-
in the Aircraft Warning Center and being blood donors. In the
Women’s War Bond Drive we were responsible for the sale of
$26,700 worth of bonds. Our chapter contributed to the Red
Cross Drive and the Red Cross Kit Fund. On the average of every
five weeks we sponsor a dance for the service men. Hadassah was
responsible for a booth for tuberculosis seal sale and was success¬
ful in securing 10 tables for the Infantile Paralysis party. Manv
of our members extend hospitality to the men in service.
This year that has reached so high a mark of service in Pales¬
tine is also rich in development and service on the American
scene. In accordance with Hadassah’s program of education, the
chapter conducts an all day study group session, a monthly Oneg
Shabbat gathering and each month presents a specific phase of
Hadassah’s program by one of the committee chairmen.
□
h
Membership is the foundation upon which Hadassah’s entire is
program rests and its progress is revealed in the addition of 75 =
new members during the past year. =

This year marks the tenth anniversary of the founding of Business and Professional
Divisions of Hadassah. Within one short decade we have grown from a single division
in Detroit to 92 Divisions situated in 28 states. We have increased from a handful
of members to 5,000.
AVONDALE SUNDRY I
STORE 1
Best Line Toiletries Sundries |
SODA FOUNTAIN ¡
Our Jacksonville B. & P. Division has made a parallel growth with the national
figures, and numbers at present 50 members.
As a part of Senior Hadassah, the B. & P. Division helped fill every quota in the
various Senior Hadassah projects: Hadassah Medical Organization, Child Welfare,
Jewish National Fund, Youth Alivah, and. Palestine Supplies. The Jax B. & P. Division
has also taken an active part in USO work, sponsoring several dances, buffet suppers,
and home parties. Our members have helped with many Re’d Cross endeavors. We
have also assisted in obtaining rooms for rent in private homes to relieve the critical
housing shortage" that Jacksonville is faced with. The Jacksonville B. & P. Division
is justly proud of our constructive work in a world where so much is being torn down.
The year 1943-44 will demand even more? of our time and energy. Zionism is facing
a crucial test. Jewish immigration into Palestine will ce?ase in March, 1944, if the
final phase of the British White Paper is carried through. The end of Jewish immi¬
gration would be a blow at the very foundations of the Zionist program in Palestine
and would crush the last hope of Jews in Nazi Europe. Our weapon is an American
Zionist movement of powerful mass strength. Hadassah, which has ably proved its
ability to med crises before, is now called upon to exert extraordinary effort to achieve
a vastly greater membership. EVERY MEMBER must be stirred to a realization of
her responsibility to the Jewish people at this decisive hour. None must remain
outside the working ranks.

For two decades the Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society and Home
for the Aged has been of much service to needy people of Jacksonville,
supplying them with food, fuel, clothing, medical care and housing. The
society, through its non-interest loan fund has enabled many families to
overcome financial difficulties and re-establish themselves. During the win¬
ter transients pass through our city, and these are given food and lodging.
Each year at Passover, contributions are sent to the General Orphan
Home f or Girls in Palestine, and special food baskets are sent to state
and city institutions. In addition to the regular program of aid work, on
all major Jewish holidays, extra funds and food baskets are distributed to
indigent families.
The |
Famous Drink of Friendship
JAX BREWING COMPANY ¡
The important work of building a Home for the Aged has been retarded
due to the war, nevertheless many contributions have been received, and the
Society continues its efforts towards the fulfillment of this much needed
project. A substantial portion of the Home Funds has been invested in
War Savings Bonds, in this manner contributing to the war effort. Any¬
one interested in buying a bond now, to be used as a gift later when the
home can be built is urged to do so.
Following the needs of the times, we have contributed to the American
Red Cross. The membership has cooperated wholeheartedly with the USO
in sponsoring monthly dances at the Center for the men and women in the
armed services.
During the past year, funds for the continuation of our work were raised
from dues, individual contributions, luncheons, card parties, summer fund,
turkey project and chain card parties.
A special sick fund, “The Rose Nabin Memorial Fund,” was started in
loving memory of Rose Nabin, who dedicated her life to charity.

Philip E. Coleman
This year marks a transition in the history of the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Community Council. Recognizing the
need for intensified community organization, Jacksonville
leadership has taken the initial step to create the means
whereby the Council may pass from the stage of a part-
time agency with limited scope to that of a full-fledged
functional agency with potentialities for future growth.
Heretofore, the community council has been chiefly a
fund-raising instrument for conducting the annual cam¬
paign in behalf of local, national, and overseas agencies,
although public relations, too, has been its function.
The Council has done well in these fields. 1943 marked
an all-time high point in Jacksonville’s giving. Despite
the handicap of having lost many men to the service, in¬
cluding the largest 1942 contributor, and the lessened
manpower upon which to draw for volunteer solicitation,
the 1943 Council campaign raised the largest sum of
money from the largest number of contributors.
Community Spokesman
The two services of fund-raising and public relations,
essential though they may be, are not the end all and be
all in the Jewish communal structure. They do not in
themselves make for a complete Jewish community life.
The leadership of the Council has recognized this fact in
planning for a full-time Council with professional direc¬
tion.
Although the structure for a revamped organization
with broader purposes remains to be built at community
wide conferences to be called in the fall, there already
is manifest a healthy interest in having such a revision.
It should be understood, of course, that none of the me¬
chanics of community organization can in itself provide a
panacea for every ill in Jewish life. What then, will be
accomplished through an expanded vigorous community
council organized on a democratic basis?
it voluntarily by the members of the entire Jewish com¬
munity themselves. Today in Jacksonville there are im¬
portant areas of Jewish life which are neglected because
of the absence of centralized community planning.
Centralized Planning
To illustrate, because there was an obvious need for a
non-commercial publication which would mirror Jewish
problems, and mold community thinking, the Council re¬
cently began the publication of a bi-weekly newspaper,
which is being sent into every Jewish home in Jackson¬
ville. Thus for the first time the largest possible number
of Jews are being exposed to some greater understanding
of the Jewish scene, and more particularly, of the agencies
which their contributions are supporting through the an¬
nual Council Campaign.
To cite another example, there appears to be a need
for a Jewish Community forum which will periodically
provide the influence of outside Jewish personalities so
necessary in stimulating local Jewish communal activity.
The Council is planning to meet this need. There are
gaps in other aspects of local Jewish community life. We
do not, for instance, know with certainty so elementary
a fact as the number of Jews in Jacksonville. Such
knowledge is basic to normal community growth and it
should be somebody’s concern to gather the facts. Other'
wise everybody’s business becomes nobody’s business.
For the Community Council to launch such projects
successfully will require the cooperation of all sections
of the Jewish community working and thinking and plan¬
ning together. The scope and usefulness of the Council
will grow in proportion to the desire of the Jacksonville
Jewish people for making their community life as a whole
move forward in accordance with some degree of unity
and consistency over a period of years. Community or¬
ganization has become a scientific process whose principles
can be applied successfully to Jacksonville even as they
have elsewhere.
It can become, over a period of years, the responsible
community voice on all issues affecting the welfare of
the Jewish community as a whole. Up to now there has
been no such authoritative voice with powers vested in
Page Seventy
Finally, it should be stressed that the Council in its
planning aims to supersede no existing organization but
to supplement and help them move along the line of the
greatest good for the greatest number of people.

OFFICERS
President Ira Stein

Secretary Marvin Shulman
(From annual report of the Army and Navy committee)
In reviewing the work of the Army and Navy Com¬
mittee for this past year, I think it will be of interest
to recapitulate the steps that were taken in laying the
ground work that enabled us to successfully carry on
our program and expand our activities. The Jacksonville
section of the J. W. B., a USO agency, through its Army
and Navy Committee, is made up of representatives of
18 Jacksonville Jewish organizations and was organized
in February, 1941. Representing all phases of Jewish
life: Orthodox, Conservative and Reform, the J. W. B. is
the civilian agency responsible for the Jewish men in
the armed forces during their time away from camp.
Immediately upon being organized, the Army and Navy
Committee adopted a concrete program whereby we were
able to serve the men of the armed forces at all times,
and place at their disposal spiritual and recreational pro¬
grams.
IRA STEIN
| RELIGIOUS ACTIVITIES
| With the assignment of a Jewish Chaplain to Camp
= Blanding, during October of 1941, the necessity of civilian
5 Rabbis conducting services at Camp Blanding was elim-
| inated. However, services for sailors at the Naval Air
| Station are still being conducted by Rabbi Kaplan when-
| ever arrangements can be made with the naval authori-
| ties. Friday night services, followed by a social hour, are
= held each Friday night and the Jewish men of the armed
1 forces are urged to attend.
During the Passover Holidays of 1942, the religious
| committee again arranged for congregational Seders to be
| held at the Jewish Center and also for holiday dinners
| to be served during both afternoons of Passover. About
= 500 service men attended these Sedorim and dinners as
| guests of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. All expenses
1 were paid by members of the congregation. In addition
| to the services held at the Jewish Center, a Seder Service
| was arranged by the Jewish Temple for the first night
= of Passover and was well attended. In cooperation with
| Chaplain Chas. S. Freedman, a Seder Service was held at
| Camp Blanding for those men of Jewish faith who were
= unable to leave the camp. All of the necessary food was
§j prepared in Jacksonville and sent to the camp.
The Home Hospitality Committee meets every Sunday
morning to take care of last minute requests of service
men for homes to which they can go for a Sunday dinner.
SOCIAL ACTIVITIES
Our Social Affairs Committee has arranged weekly
dances and buffet suppers for each Saturday night during
the year. These affairs, sponsored by the Jewish organ¬
izations of Jacksonville, are held in the Auditorium of
the Jewish Center and the Vestry Room of the Jewish
Temple and have proved very popular with the service
men throughout the year. There are also small house par¬
ties arranged through the cooperation of the business and
Professional group of Hadassah and Junior Hadassah. On
each Sunday afternoon Open House is held in the Audi¬
torium of the Jewish Center under the sponsorship of
our Social Affairs Committee, Junior Hadassah and the
Daughters of Israel. These affairs are informal—the girls
and service men creating their own entertainment.
In March, 1942, an organization of Jewish girls of Jack¬
sonville was formed. This organization is known as the
“Military Maids,” and now has a membership of over 100
girls. They cooperate in all social affairs sponsored by the
Army and Navy Committee.
1 For Sh’vous of 1942 invitations were mailed to all of
| the known Jewish soldiers and sailors to attend services
| at both the Jewish Temple and the Jewish Center. There
| were about 250 men at the two congregations. After the
i services these men were invited to Jewish homes for
| dinner.
During the High Holy Days of 1942, arrangements
| were made for the men of Jewish faith to secure leave
| from camp in order to attend religious services at Jack-
I sonville. About 650 Jewish soldiers and sailors were in
| the city for Rosh Hashonah. As the holiday was from
= Friday night through Sunday night this provided quite
1 a problem, for sleeping accomodations over the week ends
| are at a premium. However, reservations for over 300
^ men had been made in advance at various tourist homes
= and lodging houses. In addition there were over 100
= men taken care of in private homes of our Jewish
| citizens. During this period over 450 Jewish soldiers
| and sailors received invitations for home hospitality at
| the homes of members of our two Jewish congregations.
ñ Dinner was also arranged for 172 Jewish service men at
1 a local Jewish restaurant.
PERSONAL SERVICE
Wives of service men who came to Jacksonville to join
their husbands were given help in finding apartments or
furnished rooms and in some instances were helped to
find employment. It is also interesting to note that the
J. W. B. was called upon by civilian defense workers to
help them find living quarters. Many of these defense
workers were successfully assisted.
JWB-YMHA ACTIVITIES
Jacksonville’s newly reorganized YMHA opened its doors
to the Jewish community on May 28, 1942. This building
immediately became the second home for the Jewish
service men. Equipped with modern lounge, writing
rooms, library, billiard room, ping pong room, game
rooms, gymnasium and shower rooms the YMHA’ imme¬
diately became popular with the men and women in uni¬
form. With the assistance of the JWB professional worker
it was arranged to convert the Y. gymnasium into sleep¬
ing quarters over the week ends. We are now able to
accomodate 45 service men on Saturday nights.
| HOME HOSPITALITY
| Hundreds of Jacksonville Jewish families have opened
| their homes to the soldiers and sailors of Jewish faith
| and the same men are invited to return whenever they
| are in the city. Our committee aids those men who have
| not as yet made personal contact with Jewish families.
Each Wednesday night a special USO program is held
at the YMHA in connection with the JWB. These Wed¬
nesday night affairs have proven very popular and we
have had some very interesting amateur shows produced
with soldier talent.
To all of you who have assisted in this splendid work
the committee expresses its appreciation.

B’NAI B’RITH AUXILIARY
After several years of preliminary
organizational efforts, the Women’s
Auxiliary of the Adolph B. Weil Lodge
of B’Nai B’Rith finally came into ex¬
istence on November 18, 1942, when
its charter was formally presented to it
by Mrs. Louis Ossinsky, vice-president
of District Five Women’s Auxiliary.
Mrs. Irving Levin acted as temporary
president during its organizational ef¬
forts, after which she was succeeded
by Mrs. Max L. Friedberg, the present
president.
Although the organization is only
several months of age, it has already
achieved outstanding success in sev¬
eral of its projects. Its most marked
success came with its designation of
having led all organizations in the
City of Jacksonville in the project of
furnishing Company Day Rooms at
Camp Blanding, by having completely
furnished four company day rooms.
This project was under the joint
chairmanship of Mrs. Irving Levin and
Mrs. Philip Selber. Most of the fur¬
nishings were secured through the ac¬
tive efforts of the ladies in collecting
used articles of furniture and reclaim¬
ing them for use in the Day Rooms.
In addition to the Company Day
Room project, the auxiliary also aided
in the furnishing of the new Hillel
Home at Gainesville, Florida, and has
aided the Lodge i'n some of its own
projects.
A very bright future is predicted
for this newest of all Jewish organiza¬
tions in Jacksonville. Plans for an even
greater achievement are now being
made.

MARION HERTZMAN
Military Maids was organized for the purpose af assisting the Jewish
| Welfare Board in their work with service men and women. They act as
| Junior Hostesses at the regular Saturday Night Dances given at the Jewish
1 Center and Jewish Temple. On Sundays they regularly attend a reception
| and tea dance. On Wednesday nights the Military Maids have charge of
| home hospitality at the Y. M. H. A. where dancing, ping pong, and playing
| pool are enjoyed. On Thursday nights they attend weekly dances at Starke,
5
| acting as junior hostesses.
| The Military Maids also assisted the Jewish Welfare Board in making
1 plans for the Passover holidays.

| Junior Hadasah, the outstanding
| Zionist youth organization in America,
has made great strides in bringing the
1 mesasge of Zion to its young women in
| Jacksonville. Realizing the critical pe¬
ll riod in which they are living, members
| of Junior Hadassah have determined
| to devote themselves to the defense of
= America, to helping the allied nations
| and to the building of a Jewish home-
¡ land in Palestine. By conducting this
55 joint Palestinian and American pro-
! gram, Junior Hadassah contributes
| daily to the war effort.
| Through the Jewish National Fund it
1 is increasing the purchase of land for
1 the settlement of more refugees and to
| raise more food for the allied nations.
| Junior Hadassah is solely responsible
| for the children’s village of Meier
H Shfeyat which has received a number
| of refugee children through the Youth
= Aliyah. Junior Hadassah has extended
55 the curriculum of its Henrietta Szold
1 School of Nursing to teach air raid pre-
1 cautions and first aid to hundreds of
| nurses and volunteers. Pardess Anna,
2 the orange groves, is another project
= for which Junior Hadassah is solely
| responsible. Developed originally as a
1 citrus farm for Shfeyat children, it
| has now become a source of foodstuff
2 supply and the oranges are also being
| converted into chemicals used in war-
= fare.
1 The Jacksonville unit of Junior Ha-
§ dasah, under the capable leadership of
| its officers and guidance of Senior Ha-
= dassah has participated in all of these
¡5 projects. During the past year it has
| sponsored a series of Youth Aliyah
1 card parties, a raffle, the annual Yom
| Kippur dance and participated in J. N.
= F. box collections. Sponsoring a num-
¡ ber of the USO-Jewish Welfare Board
2 affairs, cooperating with the Communi-
! ty Council, leading Young Judean
2 groups, and participating in Friday
1 night services are some of the activities
E of the unit. Among the successful
= events of the year are a Donor cocktail
| party and formal dance, and a brilliant
2 membership affair. Climaxing the
Ü year’s activities was a week-end visit
§ of the national vice president, Geral-
1 dine Kassover. Miss Kassover was an
1 inspirational influence to the organiza-
Ü tion, as sguest speaker at Friday night
E services, Sabbath Oneg Shebat, an ex-
§ ecutive board meeting and a Sunday
Ü afternoon membership tea.
Junior Hadassah’s educational pro¬
gram fosters an understanding and an
appreciation of Jewish life, its rich
cultural contributions to civilization
and the contemporary ideals that mo¬
tivate Zionism. This is achieved
through the study of vital current
events and lives of great figures in
Jewish history, celebrations and obser¬
vance of Jewish holidays, moving pic¬
tures, dramatics, and Palestinian songs
and dances. The educational program
is the basis upon which the organiza¬
tion has built its plans for the coming
year. With eager anticipation, the unit
looks to the future for continued suc¬
cess and progress.

THE NATIONAL COUNCIL OF IEWISH WOMEN
The first principle of the National Council of Jewish Women, the
organization for United Jewish womanhood, is Unity. It is the only National
organization in this country which includes every type of Jewish woman—
Orthodox, Conservative, Reformed, Zionist, Non-Zionist and unaffiliated,
working together for the American Way of Life.
Our principal aims are the welfare and advancement of American women,
the betterment of conditions in our cities, states, our nation and the world.
Council exerts every effort to make its members conscious of their duties in
a democratic community.
The Jacksonville Section, in the three short years of its existence has
achieved city wide prestige and the good will and appreciation of city and
national organizations for our non-sectarian welfare work, such as Toyery,
Nursery Schools, War Service Volunteers, Hospital Visiting, our very im¬
portant work for the Foreign born, besides our legislative, religious and edu¬
cational projects.
In these times of world crisis, the Council faces an increasing demand for
service, plus an ever growing burden on its budget. Therefore, it is our aim,
for the coming year, to secure the loyal cooperation of every Jewish woman
in the community, to work untiringly for the betterment of self and fellow
man. , ,
Mrs. Oscar L. Silverman
the NATE COUNCIL JEWISH WOMEN

I. M. LIEBERMAN
Probably no similar type organization in Jacksonville
has had the dramatic history of the Young Men’s He¬
brew Association. A powerful entity in community life
for years, post-war (World War No. 1) conditions com¬
pelled the institution to cease its functions.
Then in 1937, a new organization was formed—the
Young Men’s Club—with the express purpose of re¬
viving the YMHA, without which no Jewish community
in any large city can find its best expression.
Beginning with a charter membership of less than
thirty, the Young Men’s Club grew rapidly into an or¬
ganization comprising almost two hundred represent¬
ative men of the Jewish community, attesting to the
widespread interest in the project and confirming the
belief that Jacksonville was ready for a new “Y”. Trus¬
tees of the assets of the old “Y” collaborated eagerly,
and the dream came true.
Early in 1942 Jacksonville’s new YMHA became a
fact, occupying a building adjoining the Jewish Center
on Third Street. The 220 members comprised charter
members, regular members and junior members and
represented en toto the entire Jewish community.
Under the able direction of the organization’s first
president, Mr. Ira Stein, the “Y” began to function as
a community center and focal point for many important
Jewish activities. Equipped with game rooms, billiard
tables, a lounge room, meeting rooms, and partially
equipped gymnasium, the “Y’s” activities, however, be¬
came subordinated to the affairs of a nation at war.
Soon, 40 members of the “Y” had enlisted or been
called to colors. Wartime endeavors took precedence
over all others. Jacksonville, as a city with Air Train¬
ing Stations and the focal point for thousands of men
in training at Camp Blanding nearby, had the problem
of playing host to servicemen. The “Y” directors quickly
recognized that, for the wartime period at least, “busi¬
ness as usual” was out. Within a short while, the “Y”
was transferred almost completely into a servicemen’s
center, with all the facilities of the house open to ser¬
vicemen, and with week-end sleeping accomodations
provided for many of them, in conjunction with the
USO-JWB. At the present writing there are accomoda¬
tions for 45 men, and more will be added as soon as
feasible.
On June 29th, in annual session, the organization met
to nominate and elect new officers. Mr. Ira Stein relin¬
quished the presidency to Mr. I. M. Lieberman whose
versatile and productive role in Jacksonville’s Jewish
community life needs no elaboration. Also elected were
a full set of officers and a board of directors of 17.
“The war which has disrupted our plans for a greater
YMHA,” said Mr. Lieberman, “will soon be over, God
willing. We shall have preserved our rights to life,
liberty and the pursuit of happiness, and it is my earn¬
est and sincere hope—which I have no doubt will be
fulfilled—that the Jacksonville YMHA will take its
rightful place in community life.”

HOLIDAY
PROGRESSIVE CREDIT UNION
GREETINGS
The Progressive Credit Union is a saving and loan organization
¡ for mutual benefits, one of a group of about 12,000 with a total
| membership of over 3,000,000 in the United States, chartered Feb-
¡ ruary, 1940, under the supervision of the Florida State Banking De-
I partment. Since that date the Progressive Credit Union, sponsored
¡ by the Jewish Progressive Society, has been rendering a great service
| to the progressive and liberal minded people of this community.
In a letter from the Comptroller’s office of the State of Florida,
I dated April 15, 1942, is found the following observation: “This credit
¡ union is different, it is in conformity with both the letter and spirit of
| the law governing credit unions.”
Every member of the Board of Directors and other active mem-
I bers of the organization have excelled in the services each has ren-
I dered.
All work is performed without compensation other than the
| knowledge of service to one’s fellowmen. Expenses paid out are for
1 the following services: (a) Insurance, (b) Treasurer’s Bond, (c) Bank
¡ Charges, (d) Examination Tax, and (e) Credit Union League mem-
1 bership. All other expenses are paid by the Jewish Progressive
1 Society.

September 1—Junior Hadassah Yom Kippur Dance.
October 7—Daughters of Israel Opening Luncheon enjoyed by many.
October 11—Sunday School Registration.
October 13—Senior Hadassah Opening Meeting and Thrift Luncheon has large
attendance.
October 20—Ladies’ Aid Society Opening Luncheon successful.
October 27—Daughters of Israel have successful card party.
November 11—Zionist District installs Harry Katz as president. I. M. Lieberman
and Rev. J. Robin honored for outstanding service to organization.
November 13—Debate on timely subject followed late services. Enjoyed by large
congregation.
November 20—Harold S. Cohen, editor of Jacksonville Journal, speaks at late
services.
November 18—B’Nai B’Rith Auxiliary initiated with impressive ceremonies at joint
meeting of B’Nai B’Rith and Auxiliarly. Rabbi Amram Prero and
Mrs. J. Goldstrom guests of the evening.
November 24—Ladies Aid Society enjoy bridge party.
November 20—Harold S. Cohn, editor of Jacksonville Journal, speaks at late
November 27—Special Thanksgiving Service held. Choir renders appropriate
selections.
December 6—Jacksonville Jewish Center has elaborate Chanukah Dinner. Rabbi
S. Benjamin guest speaker and Hyman Silverstein, master of cere¬
monies.
December 9—Joint meeting of Senior, Junior and B. & P. W. Hadassah enjoyed
by many.
December 31—Daughters of Israel sponsor brilliant New Year’s affair.
January 10—Center Mortgage is paid off.
January 21—Pregressive Credit Union holds annual banquet for membership.
January 24—Jacksonville Zionist District announces opening of Bond Drive.
January 27—Senior Hadassah Membership Tea is lovely event.
February 5—Troop 14, Boy Scouts of America, sponsor late service.
February 12—Annual Daughters of Israel Sabbath enjoys large congregation.
February 13—A. Z. A. Fraternity‘sponsors successful Valentine Dance.
February 28—Daughters of Israel have 28th annual affair. Show, “Hi-Di-Ho” en¬
joyed by many.
March 10—Senior Hadassah holds Linen Shower. Large amount of linens sent to
Palestine.
March 21—Purim Affairs sponsored by the Center and Jewish Welfare Board. Max
Auerbach and Rabbi M. D. Margolis speakers.
April 2—Center-Temple Joint Service. Dr. Abram Sachar, national head of
Hillel, guest speaker, enjoyed by large gathering.
April 9—Dr. Norman Salit, author and lecturer, guest speaker at Friday night
services. Large congregation attends.
April 13—Dr. Nacham Yud addresses audience at Center.
April 16—Girl Scout Troop 2 sponsors Friday night service.
April 19-20—Center conducts Passover Sedorim for service men and women.
Large number attend.
May 7—All youth groups participate in special Mother’s Day Service. Last late
service of the season.
May 12—Ludwig Lewisohn, noted author, appears before large audience at the
Center, sponsored by the lo cal Zionist District.
May 18—Center officers and Board members re-elected to serve another year.
June 6—Closing exercises of Sunday School. Prizes and awards presented to out¬
standing pupils.
June 13—Sixteen boys and girls Confirmed at impressive ceremony.
June 14—Abe Newman elected as Jacksonville District Representative to the
American Jewish Conference.
June 21-23—Zionist Youth Commission sponsors three day conference. Dr. Aharon
Kessler and Miss Helen Seff lead sessions.
August 15—A. Z. A. Fraternity sponsors annual minstrel and dance, which
proves very successful.

===== PAGE 91 =====
Same fyaetl About the ¡jmuiáti Gcdeuda/i
Though the daily life of the Jew is guided by the civil calendar, for religious purposes we have our own
calendar.
There are several reasons why the preservation of the Jewish calendar is of the utmost importance.
First, it is an ancient legacy of the Jewish people and it links us to its past. The great events of Jewish historv
are identified by their Hebrew dates. Second, the Jewish calendar is essential for the correct determination ^
of the Jewish festival dates. As long as there is Judaism, which include the observance of the Jewish festivals J
and ritual, so long will it be necessary to preserve and perpetuate the Jewish calendar. Third, the Jewish cal- gj
endar gives a distinctive character to the Jewish people. Every people and every religion developes its own fa
lorm by which it can be identified and which gives it uniqueness. The Jewish calendar gives this identity and j
uniqueness to the Jewish people.
The calendar in common use throughout the western world is based on the sun. The Mohammedans, ^
however, base their calendar upon the phases of the moon. The Jewish calendar is a compromise between the \
two, and is based both upon the sun and upon the moon. The months are reckoned according to the moon,
(12 months of 29/2 days .each), and the year according to the sun. In order to take up the extra eleven days, /
(Since the moon year lias 354 and a sun year has 365 days), a whole month is added to the calendar in a leap p
year. Every second or third year there is an extra month which is called “Ve-Adar,” and Adar. \
The Jewish calendar is a very old one and its history is very obscure. We do know, however, that in an- y
cient times the Jews determined the month, year, and ¡estival entirely by the testimony offered that the moon (s
had appeared and had been seen. The Sanhedrin was given the authority to hear this testimony and through v
it to establish the beginning of the month, the intercalation of the calendar, and the dates of the festivals. At J
first the New Moon was announced through the lighting of fires on hilltops. Later, messengers were sent iij
out to proclaim the date. However, since it took time for these messengers of the Sanhedrin to announce the ^
New Moon to communities outside of Palestine, it was decreed that these communities were to observe the ^
festivals for two days instead of one. This added day was called, “The second holiday of the Diaspora/' An P
exception was made in the case of Yom Kippur, which, because of the hardship of fasting could not be pro- J
longed. Rosli Hashonah was observed for two days even in Palestine because it was also the New Moon, the ^
first day of Tishri. In still later times, the astronomical calculation of the calendar became so precise that the \
practice of hearing witnesses about the appearance of the moon was discarded. Despite this, the second day ^
of the festival in the Diaspora is still observed by Orthodox and Conservative Jews. /
Some of the Jewish months have 29 days and some have 30 days. The 30 day months have two days f
Rosh Chodesh (beginning of the month), but the first day of the new month begins with the second day of J
Rosh Chodesh. ^
In order to complete the adjustment of the lunar with the solar calendar the months of Heshvan and r*
Kislev are irregular in length, sometimes having 29 and sometimes 30 days. >
This adjustment also prevents the Day of Atonement from ever occuring either on a Friday or on a Sun- /
day, so that the preparation of food may not be disturbed because of the immediate proximity of a Fast Day to
the Sabbath.
The Jewish day begins at sundown and ends the following sundown. Shabbos therefore begins at sun¬
down Friday night, and Friday is called “Erev Shabbos", the eve of Sabbath. Holidays begin at sundown of
the day before the first day of the holiday and the day called “Erev Yom Tov," the eve of the holiday. In
order to be certain that the Sabbaths and holidays are properly observed, it is essential that the candles be
kindled at least 15 minutes before sundown.
The Jewish calendar appearing in the following pages is arranged according to the civil calendar. In the
upper right hand comer of each month are the names of the Hebrew month corresponding to the English
month in the upper left hand corner. The small numeral in the lower left corner of each box is the date of
the Hebrew month. , ,v ,
Finally, in place of the name of the Hebrew selection which is read on Saturday, this calendar gives the
chapters and verses which are read from the Torah on Saturday. We hope that this will give an incentive to
some of the readers to read the weekly Bible lesson every Saturday.

¡ JACKSONVILLE JEWISH COMMUNITY COUNCIL SPONSORS
¡ CULTURAL SERIES
= The Jewish community of Jacksonville for the first time in its history will have
= a Jewish cultural series during- the coming season, according- to an announcement by
a Hyman Selber, chairman of a special committee of the Jacksonville Jewish Community
= Council which is inaugurating the series as part of its expanded service to the com-
! munity.
s
Oragnizations are asked to note the following dates of the cultural events, so that
= they will refrain from scheduling conflicting affairs.
E MARVIN LOWENTHAL—Wednesday, November 3, Jewish Center.
= Historian, lecturer, foreign correspondent, author of “Life and Letters of
= Henrietta Szold.”
E2
= IRVING DAVIDSON—Tuesday, December 7, Temple Aliavatli Chesed.
= Humorist and collector of Jewish wit, known as the “Jewish Ripley.”
H FILM FESTIVAL—Tuesday, January 11, Jewish Center.
= An evening of Jewish film classics.
= DR. A. A. NEUMAN—Tuesday, February 8, Temple Aliavath Chesed.
Rabbi, author, editor, president of Dropsie College, author of “Jews in Spain.”
= MICHAEL AND FRIE DAI AN—Tuesday, March 7, Jewish Center.
Pianist-narrator and baritone, presenting- “A Cavalcade of Jewish Music.”
= All of the above programs beg’in at 8:15 p. m. Season tickts are available at the
j= office of the Council, 7F Atlantic National Bank Annex or at the Center Office.
j§ The price for the entire series or five events is $2.00, including the federal tax.
CENTER NOW SERVES ITS
SOUTH SIDE MEMBERSHIP
MORE ADEQUATELY
Parents of children from the South
Side who plan to atttend the Hebrew
School maintained by the Center will
be pleased to learn that the Center has
purchased a new bus and increased its
facilities for transporting the children
from that area to and from the Center
for instruction.
In view of this added convenience,
the officers of the Center anticipate a
sharp rise in enrollment from the
South Side.
The institution also is continuing the
nursery and kindergarten which was
such a successful innovation of the
1942-43 season.
Morris Bar-Am of New Haven,
Conn, has taken up his new duties as
principal of the Sunday School and in¬
structor of Hebrew. He has had 18
years of experience in teaching and
should prove a valuable addition to
the Center’s educational program.

“We are pleased to recommend the work of the Southern Monument Company to the members of
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Mr. Charles Speers, president, has cooperated at all times with the
Cemetary Committee by erecting only monuments of the finest workmanship and which conform to
our regulations.” — MAX RUBIN, Chmn., Cemetary Comm.
